<div id="review_68536155" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/68536155" />
    <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely"><img alt="K.D. Absolutely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471967140p2/2156200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/68536155?book_show_action=true">Aug 23, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="K.D. Absolutely" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely">K.D. Absolutely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780451527912" href="/book/show/1124745.The_House_of_the_Seven_Gables">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to K.D. by: </span>
      1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die (2006-2010)
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=1001-core">1001-core</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=gothic">gothic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer68536155" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3131340136047179293">An old US colloquial house with seven gables that seem to be mocking heaven. Seven main characters. The old ugly <b>Hepzibah Pyncheon</b> running a candy shop to earn a living for herself and her war-torn brother <b>Clifford Pyncheon</b>. Her face is ugly because she has to squint to see. She needs to wear eye-glasses but she is so poor that she cannot afford to have one. So customers are few except the young adorable boy <b>Ned Higgins</b> who loves gingerbread cookies that he comes back again and again to the cand</span>
  <span id="freeText3131340136047179293" style="display:none">An old US colloquial house with seven gables that seem to be mocking heaven. Seven main characters. The old ugly <b>Hepzibah Pyncheon</b> running a candy shop to earn a living for herself and her war-torn brother <b>Clifford Pyncheon</b>. Her face is ugly because she has to squint to see. She needs to wear eye-glasses but she is so poor that she cannot afford to have one. So customers are few except the young adorable boy <b>Ned Higgins</b> who loves gingerbread cookies that he comes back again and again to the candy shop ignoring Hepzibah's face. Hovering in the background is the lone tenant, the daguerreotypist (old style photographer) <b>Holgrave</b> that stuck it up in the house for an unknown reason.<br /><br />Then the other characters come to this old decrepit decaying house one at a time as if Hawthorne is calling them up the stage one by one: the young beautiful <b>Phoebe Pyncheon</b> stepping on the old wooden porch, the cunning and greedy <b>Judge Jaffrey Pyncheon</b> insisting to go inside the house despite protestations from Phoebe, and the frequent visitor who lives nearby, <b>Uncle Venner</b>.<br /><br />This makes this novel remarkable: <u>the characters</u>. Hawthorne has this masterful skill in providing contracts to his characters by highlighting their contrasts, e.g., the ugly but good-hearted Hepzibah vs the sweet-smiling but cunning Jaffrey, the young lovely Phoebe stepping on the porch of the decrepit old house, the young adorable Ned chewing the gingerbread cookies given to him by scary lady, etc. The house with glorious past hiding a dark secret. Images that are so stark and vivid that will stay with you as you close the book.<br /><br />The scare here is not due to a boy and a black man being able to read other people's mind or tell what will happen next. Nor from a lady in the bath tub whose blood-smeared breasts float on the water in a bath tub. The scare here comes from realization that a man's greed that happened long time ago can have an effect to the next generations. You sow and you <i>and also possibly your children and grandchildren</i> reap. The scare here is about man's frailty due to money. We all know that money can be evil. And if you do not have enough of it to pay for our mounting bills, it can result to sleepless nights and can drive you and your family all crazy.<br /><br />For this reason, it's Hawthorne over King.<br /><br /><br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="3131340136047179293" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_12509216" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/12509216" />
    <a title="Jason Pettus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/147289-jason-pettus"><img alt="Jason Pettus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548377232p2/147289.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/12509216?book_show_action=true">Jan 14, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason Pettus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason Pettus" href="/user/show/147289-jason-pettus">Jason Pettus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer12509216" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12389514402375763979">(My full review of this book is much larger than GoodReads' word-count limitations. Find the entire essay at the Chicago Center for Literature and Photography [cclapcenter.com].)<br/><br/><b>The CCLaP 100:</b> In which I read 100 supposed "classics" for the first time, then write reports on whether or not I think they deserve the label<br/><br/>Book #2: <i>House of the Seven Gables</i>, by Nathaniel Hawthorne<br/><br/><i>The story in a nutshell:</i><br/>Like any good horror story, the spooky <i>House of the Seven Gables</i> actually tells two stories at onc</span>
  <span id="freeText12389514402375763979" style="display:none">(My full review of this book is much larger than GoodReads' word-count limitations. Find the entire essay at the Chicago Center for Literature and Photography [cclapcenter.com].)<br /><br /><b>The CCLaP 100:</b> In which I read 100 supposed "classics" for the first time, then write reports on whether or not I think they deserve the label<br /><br />Book #2: <i>House of the Seven Gables</i>, by Nathaniel Hawthorne<br /><br /><i>The story in a nutshell:</i><br />Like any good horror story, the spooky <i>House of the Seven Gables</i> actually tells two stories at once: it is simultaneously the historic tale of the cursed Pyncheon family, concurrent owners of a reputedly haunted house in Salem, Massachusetts for over two centuries now, as well as the specific tale of the most recent generation of this family, dealing with the same curse that has haunted all the Pyncheons since Puritan times. It seems that the original owner of the seven-gabled house, old Colonel Pyncheon, ended up getting a man named Maule killed as a witch in order to weasel out of the construction costs of the house itself, even deliberately knowing that the man was innocent; Maule, it seems, as a result issued an infamous curse on the Pyncheon family as he died, one that has haunted any member in those two centuries who's had anything to do with the house in question. In the meanwhile, though, another persistent rumor has been that the Pyncheon family actually owns a <i>whole</i> lot more land in Salem than the simple Seven Gables estate, and that if they could simply find the 200-year-old evidence then they could get the state government to retroactively reimburse them and make them rich, rich, stinkin' filthy rich; and in that respect, <i>House of the Seven Gables</i> is as much a morality tale as it is a horror or haunted-house one, in that any Pyncheon over the decades who takes an interest in finding this old evidence just ends up obsessed with the subject to their ultimate ruin, as surely as the supposed magical curse that also exists, along the tormented ghosts of all those cursed Pyncheons who still supposedly reside within the house's walls.<br /><br />Like I said, as a result the book ends up telling two stories at once, with the majority of it dedicated to the current Pyncheon family at the time of the story itself (mid-1800s): bitter spinster Hepzibah, for example, who has ended up having to open a cent-store on the first floor (basically the Victorian equivalent of a convenience store) in order to make ends meet; her elderly brother Clifford, a broken sad-sack who has just gotten out of jail after spending 30 years there for a crime he didn't commit; Judge Jaffrey, a haughty and hard old man who is thinking of running for governor, and who has become convinced that Clifford knows where the hidden Pyncheon real-estate evidence is; and the sweet-as-sunshine Young Phoebe, a rural cousin who is visiting that summer in order to help out this terminally dour family, and who is like a freakin' little rainbow compared to the rest of the family's endless thunderstorms. Combine with a lot of melodrama, a series of events that are semi-supernatural in nature, and a liberal sprinkling of backstory about the doomed Pyncheons of yore, and you have yourself one very Victorian novel indeed.<br /><br /><i>The argument for it being a classic:</i>...</span>
  <a data-text-id="12389514402375763979" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_73657170" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/73657170" />
    <a title="Stuart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/865173-stuart"><img alt="Stuart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1229622675p2/865173.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/73657170?book_show_action=true">Oct 06, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stuart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stuart" href="/user/show/865173-stuart">Stuart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer73657170" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1256238885997195593">Another "great American novel" that really is one of the Great American Novels, this book is a surprisingly quick read, by turns charming and creepy, with a small but excellently drawn cast of characters ranging from the comically tragic but dignified Hepzibah, to the gracefully mysterious Holgrave. An unexpected plot twist at the beginning of the book's final third leads to two chapters of excellent writing, one detailing Clifford and Hepzibah's flight on a train and the almost psychotic breakd</span>
  <span id="freeText1256238885997195593" style="display:none">Another "great American novel" that really is one of the Great American Novels, this book is a surprisingly quick read, by turns charming and creepy, with a small but excellently drawn cast of characters ranging from the comically tragic but dignified Hepzibah, to the gracefully mysterious Holgrave. An unexpected plot twist at the beginning of the book's final third leads to two chapters of excellent writing, one detailing Clifford and Hepzibah's flight on a train and the almost psychotic breakdown Clifford experiences while talking to a fellow traveler (the whole scene is very much in the vein of CRIME AND PUNISHMENT) and another detailing the passage of time from the perspective of a corpse. Smaller characters pop up in deftly crafted portraits and fantastic and wise passages and quotes litter the novel like gems in a treasure trove of gold coins. A definite must read for everyone interested in early American literature, gothic romances and good books all around. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1256238885997195593" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1080719205" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1080719205" />
    <a title="Tristram Shandy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18181298-tristram-shandy"><img alt="Tristram Shandy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589454223p2/18181298.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1080719205?book_show_action=true">Oct 15, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tristram Shandy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tristram Shandy" href="/user/show/18181298-tristram-shandy">Tristram Shandy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">fervent penitents</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18181298-tristram-shandy?shelf=classic-american-literature">classic-american-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18181298-tristram-shandy?shelf=clearly-overestimated">clearly-overestimated</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1080719205" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18246196724023456393"><b>A Never-Ending Story</b><br/><br/>Whereas Hawthorne, in <i>The House of the Seven Gables</i>, wanted to imply that greed and dishonesty, following their selfish ends with the help of established power, may well be said to create a sense of guilt which will stretch over and deform several generations, thus beginning a never-ending story of vice, uneasiness and retribution, reading the novel (or, as the author will have it, the Romance) itself soon seemed like a futile attempt at emulating Sisyphos to me. To be sure, </span>
  <span id="freeText18246196724023456393" style="display:none"><b>A Never-Ending Story</b><br /><br />Whereas Hawthorne, in <i>The House of the Seven Gables</i>, wanted to imply that greed and dishonesty, following their selfish ends with the help of established power, may well be said to create a sense of guilt which will stretch over and deform several generations, thus beginning a never-ending story of vice, uneasiness and retribution, reading the novel (or, as the author will have it, the Romance) itself soon seemed like a futile attempt at emulating Sisyphos to me. To be sure, I have always enjoyed Hawthorne’s short stories for their power of language and their capacity of conjuring up a sense of the supernatural and unknown, and so I finally was persuaded to give <i>The House of the Seven Gables</i> a try although I remember from my university days the seemingly endless hours I invested in having it out with <i>The Scarlett Letter</i>. However, as I had read some of his short stories after the agony of Hester Prynne (and the unsuspecting reader) I came to the conclusion that I was just too young to appreciate that novel and that, after all, maybe it was just me and not Nathaniel Hawthorne.<br /><br /><i>The House of the Seven Gables</i> now taught me a different, rather soothing lesson:<br /><br /><u>It was, after all, Nathaniel Hawthorne, and not me!</u><br /><br />The story of an old crime that set at variance two families, the well-to-do and influential Pyncheons and the underdog Maules who are rumoured to practise witchcraft, and that haunts their family histories for nearly two centuries is hampered from the very beginning by Hawthorne’s tendency to drown his subject-matter in what might be called a torrent of words – were it not for the fact that a torrent is usually characterized by swift movement which carries everything along with it. Therefore I’d rather call Hawthorne’s technique dipping his readers into a brine lake of words.<br /><br />The whole thing starts interestingly enough with an overview on the history of the eminent Pyncheon family and the appropriation of the piece of land on which the House of the Seven Gables was to be built, and it goes on to the mysterious and apparently supernatural death of the family patriarch, but already in the second chapter we are given a briny description of the little cent shop that old Hepzibah Pyncheon sets up to increase her small income for the sake of her brother Clifford, who is released from prison after serving a thirty-year sentence for murder. Further descriptions will be given of the garden and even of the fowl that inhabit this garden – and Mr. Hawthorne leaves no doubt as to the symbolic potential of this poultry. Here we are at the root of the problem for nearly everything about the Pyncheon mansion has a symbolic meaning: There is a sickly well, there are roses looking nice from afar but if you inspect them more closely you will find them covered with mildew, there are Alice’s Posies which, conveniently, start to bloom on a very special occasion, and while you are reading the endless paragraphs concerned with these symbols you cannot help but feel annoyed with Mr. Hawthorne for spelling it out so neatly like a self-important schoolmaster. I repeatedly – having time enough – asked myself, “Does the author take his readers for morons, or is he so pleased with his own symbolism that he has to rub it in over and over again?” Whichever it is – it is the cause of an uninspiredly repetitive and needless verbosity that made me come to the conclusion that Hawthorne is for American prose what Whitman is for American poetry: time-consuming, self-enamored and enervating.<br /><br />What made me go through with the novel for all its lengthiness then? To be fair, it is not all that bad because sometimes the dialogues pick up the pith I know from Mr. Hawthorne’s short stories and there are curious ideas like the following:<br /><br /><blockquote>”’Shall we never, never get rid of this Past?’ cried he, keeping up the earnest tone of his preceding conversation. ‘It lies upon the Present like a giant's dead body In fact, the case is just as if a young giant were compelled to waste all his strength in carrying about the corpse of the old giant, his grandfather, who died a long while ago, and only needs to be decently buried. Just think a moment, and it will startle you to see what slaves we are to bygone times,—to Death, if we give the matter the right word!’ <br /><br />‘But I do not see it,’ observed Phoebe. <br /><br />‘For example, then,’ continued Holgrave: ‘a dead man, if he happens to have made a will, disposes of wealth no longer his own; or, if he die intestate, it is distributed in accordance with the notions of men much longer dead than he. A dead man sits on all our judgment-seats; and living judges do but search out and repeat his decisions. We read in dead men's books! We laugh at dead men's jokes, and cry at dead men's pathos! We are sick of dead men's diseases, physical and moral, and die of the same remedies with which dead doctors killed their patients! We worship the living Deity according to dead men's forms and creeds. Whatever we seek to do, of our own free motion, a dead man's icy hand obstructs us! Turn our eyes to what point we may, a dead man's white, immitigable face encounters them, and freezes our very heart! And we must be dead ourselves before we can begin to have our proper influence on our own world, which will then be no longer our world, but the world of another generation, with which we shall have no shadow of a right to interfere. I ought to have said, too, that we live in dead men's houses; as, for instance, in this of the Seven Gables!’</blockquote><br /><br />These passages are few and far between but nevertheless they are like oases of green amidst an arid plain covered with – pardon the stupid pun but I think reading the whole book earned me a right to it – hawthorn. Then there is also Chapter 13, in which Holgrave reads out the story of Alice Pyncheon to the bird-brained Phoebe, and here again we have Hawthorne at his best – presenting a short story of atmospheric depth and haunting qualities. The death of the nasty old Judge and the next morning, when a number of people try to gain access to the House of the Seven Gables are also craftily realized but then Hawthorne soon overdoes it, riding his idea to death, probably because he liked it so well, and using 1,000,000 words where 1,000 words would have done the job admirably.<br /><br />A propos 1,000 words – I’m going to take a good look into the mirror and have my review end here because I think I brought the general idea across: This book has become a classic probably not because it is so very clever and complex but because it is so unpleasant and boring to read that it attracts the average literary critic, who knows that there will not be many willing to follow him into the aridity of this text. So why not apply the same principle here that is commonly applied to James Joyce’s <i>Ulysses</i> and proclaim that a book that has some idea in it but rides it to death on a craggy plain of indigestible language, thus giving you a pain in the ass, must needs be a classic? It’s probably nothing but that self-same attitude of intellectual snobbery that made Mr. Hawthorne write <i>The House of the Seven Gables</i> in the first place.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18246196724023456393" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_125672884" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/125672884" />
    <a title="John David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4357283-john-david"><img alt="John David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1581440570p2/4357283.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/125672884?book_show_action=true">Oct 10, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John David" href="/user/show/4357283-john-david">John David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/7724390-the-house-of-the-seven-gables">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4357283-john-david?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer125672884" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12466452218409476319">This review contains spoilers.<br/><br/>I have a vague memory of reading “The Scarlet Letter” sometime in middle school, and coming away feeling like you would expect after you’d read a novel about Puritan repression (that’s all I thought it was about at the time). “The House of the Seven Gables” was like finding a Hawthorne I’d never known before – one of ghosts, the eternal return of historical memory, and high Gothic romance. This time, it reminded me more of Horace Walpole and Matthew Lewis than it di</span>
  <span id="freeText12466452218409476319" style="display:none">This review contains spoilers.<br /><br />I have a vague memory of reading “The Scarlet Letter” sometime in middle school, and coming away feeling like you would expect after you’d read a novel about Puritan repression (that’s all I thought it was about at the time). “The House of the Seven Gables” was like finding a Hawthorne I’d never known before – one of ghosts, the eternal return of historical memory, and high Gothic romance. This time, it reminded me more of Horace Walpole and Matthew Lewis than it did the cold Puritanism that I once associated with Hester Prynne. In this sense, it stood up to what Hawthorne identified most of his longer fiction as, that is, “romance.” <br /><br />In the late seventeenth century, the eponymous house (actually inspired by an historical 1688 colonial mansion in Salem) served as the residence of Colonel Pyncheon, who once accused a man named Matthew Maule of sorcery in order to have him hanged, and then stole the land upon which he would eventually build his house. One day, the Colonel keels over at his desk under mysterious circumstances, but his presence and his nefariousness seem to haunt the Pyncheon house in various ways.<br /><br />Several generations later, Hepzibah and her intellectually challenged brother Clifford come to occupy the house. They are both descendants of the Colonel, but now the family fortune and good reputation have withered away so much that Hepzibah has to open a store in her house to make some extra money, and thinks herself an abject failure because of it. Holgrave, a daguerrotypist, rents a room from Hepzibah upstairs. One day, a distant relation to both Clifford and Hepzibah named Phoebe Pyncheon visits and manages to quickly change the whole tenor of the house: she is able to bring vim and vigor to the Hepzibah’s failing penny shop, and she gives Clifford the companionship and attention that he needs. Just as soon as she appears, however, she leaves again and the house falls into its former dilapidated state.<br /><br />Judge Pyncheon, another member of the family and a wealthy man about town with an eminent reputation, appears at Hepzibah’s house and announces that he wants to institutionalize Clifford. The Judge claims that Clifford knows the whereabouts of certain documents that will allow him access to vast tracts of land in Maine. While waiting to talk to Clifford, the Judge dies in much the same way that the Colonel did so many generations before. Hepzibah and Clifford head to a train station to leave their outre circumstances. Later, Phoebe returns to the house with only the artist Holgrave in residence, and he admits how he has (admittedly, somewhat predictably) always loved her. Hepzibah and Clifford soon return to live there, with Phoebe having inherited all of the Judge’s ill-gotten land. Holgrave proclaims that he is himself a distant relative of Matthew Maule, so long ago accused of conjury. The House of Seven Gables, so long riven by tumult and strife, is finally exorcised by that ultimate mage, love. <br /><br />I read this mostly as a meditation on the transgressions of history and our inevitable tendency to bear them witness no matter how far removed in time we are from them, two of Hawthorne’s pet concerns. Indeed, it’s interesting how the sins of Colonel Pyncheon seem almost to take place in a prelapsarian past while at the same time having such a profound effect on the characters presently at hand. Hawthorne wonderfully blends the oppressiveness of the past with the stark newness of the present throughout the novel: the figures of the Salem witch trials (one of whom was Judge John Hathorne, Nathaniel’s great-great-grandfather, who found many of the “witches” guilty) haunt the novel in spirit, but so do all kinds of (then) modern technologies, from Holgrave’s daguerreotype, to the train that Hepzibah and Clifford use to escape the ghosts of their pasts. Published in 1851 and with the possibility of freedom from the past being central to the novel, Hawthorne might have meant for this to be, at least in some respects, a commentary on the coming Civil War. As Faulkner, another American equally concerned with the onus of history said, "The past is never dead. It's not even past."<br /><br />I enjoyed it so much I immediately picked up “The Blithedale Romance,” a review of which will be posted soon. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12466452218409476319" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1793054167?book_show_action=true">Nov 16, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Obsidian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Obsidian" href="/user/show/35942740-obsidian">Obsidian</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1793054167" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4683369129824609005"><b>Please note that I gave this book half a star and rounded it to 1 star on Goodreads.</b><br/><br/>Bah. Bah a thousand times. I have no idea why I started reading this. I think for the Halloween Book Bingo and I ended up switching it out. This thing was painful to read. I don't even know what to tell you besides if you must read this, just pace yourself since trying to force read this thing was not fun at all. At least the last 10-15 pages were just about Project Gutenberg though. I am going to complain though</span>
  <span id="freeText4683369129824609005" style="display:none"><b>Please note that I gave this book half a star and rounded it to 1 star on Goodreads.</b><br /><br />Bah. Bah a thousand times. I have no idea why I started reading this. I think for the Halloween Book Bingo and I ended up switching it out. This thing was painful to read. I don't even know what to tell you besides if you must read this, just pace yourself since trying to force read this thing was not fun at all. At least the last 10-15 pages were just about Project Gutenberg though. I am going to complain though that my library does not have this as an e-book to download, I had to read it via Overdrive which means I had to either read this via computer or my cell. I am so used to downloading my books to my Kindle for IPAD this was another reason why it took me so long to finish.<br /><br />The long and short of it about this book is following a family and their ancestral home in New England taking place in the late 1800s. At first with describing the home and how the family (Pyncheon) came to own the land that the home was built on. At first I was intrigued since it sounded like something supernatural was taking place. But then the book jumps to the current resident of the home ( Hepzibah, say that 10 times fast) and I lost interest. There are additional characters here and there, but nothing really works. The best part of the book is when Hawthorne describes the grounds and house that sits there.<br /><br />Other than the house, the whole book moves at a plodding pace.<br /><br />We have the characters of Phoebe Pyncheon who moves in with her cousin Hepzibah and of course has all of the men falling for her.<br /><br />I don't know what to say really besides the fact the flow was terrible throughout. Nothing happens and there's a lot of well maybe this is haunted (the colonel's chair) but nothing is really sad for certain. <br /><br />I wish that the setting had come more alive for me while reading this book. I just couldn't picture things well at all and had to look up pictures of the house to get things more fixed in my mind while reading.<br /><br />The ending was a big shrug from me. I am so glad I can finally stop seeing this thing on my currently reading list. </span>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/109990102?book_show_action=true">Jul 04, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer16118697010138169232">Having read Scarlet Letter in Jr High -I was surprised how much I enjoyed House of the Seven Gables. He called it a romance vs a novel; for a romance has a moral. Here the moral was the actions of past generations effect the current generation. <br/><br/>This book is a great historical novel - of changing times in New England.   <span id="freeText16118697010138169232" style="display:none">Having read Scarlet Letter in Jr High -I was surprised how much I enjoyed House of the Seven Gables. He called it a romance vs a novel; for a romance has a moral. Here the moral was the actions of past generations effect the current generation. <br /><br />This book is a great historical novel - of changing times in New England. The Puritanical ways are changing to new thought. the impact of the Salem Witch trials - having cast a web of strife for many - is now coming back to center. Greed and arrogance of ancestors - having caused harm - is having it's karmic results. There is change in class consciousness too. Change of fashion too. there are new immigrants - as seen in stereotype of the organ grinder and monkey. Cliffords shows his childlike delight of the music from an upstairs window- more changes. <br /><br />Some of my favorite scenes are set in the garden. A garden is magical when well tended. the writings about the chickens and flowers and vegis were delightful. It gave a sense of the mundane of life then. the fish monger. the little stores arising. the ways of gossip. the way houses were built. the colonial link to England - wanting to be a LORD in Maine. Political sarcasm. galore!!!<br /><br />and gothic ghosts. colorful characters. scowls and maidens. and magic. <br /> <br />A Tale of New England life - fictional - but historical non the less<br /><br /> <br /> <br /><br /> </span>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review109990102">

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('qwhU/MdTelZ3v0/fjjetEt5hwRCSBe3twjcgRJVyJ1Q4XVkNzbhR6+MbJq4vQMf78UiSLE3/40htt9RQj7SA2Q==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('YPHT/liU8MpY9BsZFDk0Xmz2k/MjDrKMcNg0e1os21/zpN4PUn/bd8xQcmi1Tl63Q9/Az/z0vCnfWMBvQOp80g==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

Eliza Lynn Linton estudió profundamente la tradición de la brujería en Inglaterra y Escocia; lo mismo hicieron, en Irlanda, Jane Wilde y, en Gales, Mary Lewes. A su lado, un buen número de autoras –de una tal «H. L.» hasta H. D. Everett− escribieron cuentos de brujas, donde exploraron el conflicto entre religión y ciencia, la condición de la mujer apartada y acosada, la sexualidad asociada a «los espíritus malignos» y —por otro lado— a la intimidación y la explotación, las relaciones entre el amor y la muerte, y la visión de la Naturaleza como una fuerza esencialmente destructora.\n\nEstas narraciones tan memorables Engaging with photographs through sound,<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText4969780875761168537\" style=\"display:none\">In Listening to Images Tina M. Campt explores a way of listening closely to photography, engaging with lost archives of historically dismissed photographs of black subjects taken throughout the black diaspora. Engaging with photographs through sound, Campt looks beyond what one usually sees and attunes her senses to the other affective frequencies through which these photographs register. She hears in these photos—which range from late nineteenth-century ethnographic photographs of rural African women and photographs taken in an early twentieth-century Cape Town prison to postwar passport photographs in Birmingham, England and 1960s mug shots of the Freedom Riders—a quiet intensity and quotidian practices of refusal. Originally intended to dehumanize, police, and restrict their subjects, these photographs convey the softly buzzing tension of colonialism, the low hum of resistance and subversion, and the anticipation and performance of a future that has yet to happen. Engaging with discourses of fugitivity, black futurity, and black feminist theory, Campt takes these tools of colonialism and repurposes them, hearing and sharing their moments of refusal, rupture,       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/7799.Nathaniel_Hawthorne">About Nathaniel Hawthorne</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer12038901060914080778">Nathaniel Hawthorne was a 19th century American novelist and short story writer. He is seen as a key figure in the development of American literature for his tales of the nation's colonial history.<br/><br/>Shortly after graduating from Bowdoin College, Hathorne changed his name to Hawthorne. Hawthorne anonymously published his first work, a novel titled <i>Fanshawe</i>, in 1828. In 1837, he published <i>Twice-Told T</i></span>
  <span id="freeText12038901060914080778" style="display:none">Nathaniel Hawthorne was a 19th century American novelist and short story writer. He is seen as a key figure in the development of American literature for his tales of the nation's colonial history.<br /><br />Shortly after graduating from Bowdoin College, Hathorne changed his name to Hawthorne. Hawthorne anonymously published his first work, a novel titled <i>Fanshawe</i>, in 1828. In 1837, he published <i>Twice-Told Tales</i> and became engaged to Sophia Peabody the next year. He worked at a Custom House and joined a Transcendentalist Utopian community, before marrying Peabody in 1842. The couple moved to The Old Manse in Concord, Massachusetts, later moving to Salem, the Berkshires, then to The Wayside in Concord. <i>The Scarlet Letter</i> was published in 1850, followed by a succession of other novels. A political appointment took Hawthorne and family to Europe before returning to The Wayside in 1860. Hawthorne died on May 19, 1864, leaving behind his wife and their three children.<br /><br />Much of Hawthorne's writing centers around New England and many feature moral allegories with a Puritan inspiration. His work is considered part of the Romantic movement and includes novels, short stories, and a biography of his friend, the United States President Franklin Pierce.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12038901060914080778" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
